<p><b>Atlantis</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἀτλαντὶς νῆσος</span>, "island of <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>") is a <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fictional</a> island mentioned within an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Hubris" title="Hubris">hubris</a> of nations in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s works <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Critias_(dialogue)" title="Critias (dialogue)">Critias</a></i>, where it represents the <a href="/wiki/Antagonist" title="Antagonist">antagonist</a> naval power that besieges "Ancient Athens", the <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual_history" title="Counterfactual history">pseudo-historic</a> embodiment of Plato's ideal state (see <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">The Republic</a></i>). In the story, Athens repels the Atlantean attack unlike any other nation of the (western) <a href="/wiki/History_of_geography#Greco-Roman_world" title="History of geography">known world</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> supposedly giving testament to the superiority of Plato's concept of a state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hackforth_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hackforth-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-David-3">[3]</a></sup> At the end of the story, Atlantis eventually falls out of favor with the deities and submerges into the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.</p>
<p>Despite its minor importance in Plato's work, the Atlantis story has had a considerable impact on literature. The allegorical aspect of Atlantis was taken up in <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> works of several <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Utopia_(book)" title="Utopia (book)">Utopia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> On the other hand, nineteenth-century amateur scholars misinterpreted Plato's narrative as historical tradition, most notably in <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_L._Donnelly" title="Ignatius L. Donnelly">Ignatius L. Donnelly</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Atlantis:_The_Antediluvian_World" title="Atlantis: The Antediluvian World">Atlantis: The Antediluvian World</a></i>. Plato's vague indications of the time of the events—more than 9,000 years before his time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>—and the alleged location of Atlantis—"beyond the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a>"—has led to much <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a> speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> As a consequence, Atlantis has become a byword for any and all supposed advanced prehistoric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_World_(genre)" title="Lost World (genre)">lost civilizations</a> and continues to inspire contemporary fiction, from comic books to films.</p>
<p>While present-day <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classicists</a> agree on the story's fictional character,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> there is still debate on what served as its inspiration. The fact that Plato borrowed some of his other allegories and metaphors—most notably the <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Gyges" title="Ring of Gyges">story of Gyges</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>—from older traditions has caused a number of scholars to investigate possible inspiration of Atlantis from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">Egyptian</a> records of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera_eruption" title="Thera eruption">Thera eruption</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> invasion, or the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luce_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luce-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Others have rejected this chain of tradition as implausible and insist that Plato created an entirely fictional nation as his example,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gill-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naddaf_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naddaf-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-17">[17]</a></sup> drawing loose inspiration from contemporary events such as the failed <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_Expedition" title="Sicilian Expedition">Athenian invasion of Sicily</a> in 415–413 BC or the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Helike" title="Helike">Helike</a> in 373 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span id="Plato's_dialogues"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Plato.27s_dialogues">Plato's dialogues</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus (dialogue)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Critias_(dialogue)" title="Critias (dialogue)">Critias (dialogue)</a></div>
<p>Plato's dialogues <i>Timaeus</i> and <i>Critias</i>, written in 360 BC, contain the earliest references to Atlantis. For unknown reasons, Plato never completed <i>Critias</i>. Plato introduced Atlantis in <i>Timaeus</i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For it is related in our records how once upon a time your State stayed the course of a mighty host, which, starting from a distant point in the Atlantic ocean, was insolently advancing to attack the whole of Europe, and Asia to boot. For the ocean there was at that time navigable; for in front of the mouth which you Greeks call, as you say, 'the pillars of Heracles,' there lay an island which was larger than Libya and Asia together; and it was possible for the travelers of that time to cross from it to the other islands, and from the islands to the whole of the continent over against them which encompasses that veritable ocean. For all that we have here, lying within the mouth of which we speak, is evidently a haven having a narrow entrance; but that yonder is a real ocean, and the land surrounding it may most rightly be called, in the fullest and truest sense, a continent. Now in this island of Atlantis there existed a confederation of kings, of great and marvelous power, which held sway over all the island, and over many other islands also and parts of the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plato_Timaeus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="736" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Plato_Timaeus.jpg/200px-Plato_Timaeus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Plato_Timaeus.jpg/300px-Plato_Timaeus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Plato_Timaeus.jpg/400px-Plato_Timaeus.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plato_Timaeus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A fifteenth-century Latin translation of Plato's <i>Timaeus</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four people appearing in those two dialogues are the politicians <a href="/wiki/Critias" title="Critias">Critias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermocrates" title="Hermocrates">Hermocrates</a> as well as the philosophers <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_of_Locri" title="Timaeus of Locri">Timaeus of Locri</a>, although only Critias speaks of Atlantis. In his works Plato makes extensive use of the <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">Socratic method</a> in order to discuss contrary positions within the context of a supposition.</p>
<p>The <i>Timaeus</i> begins with an introduction, followed by an account of the creations and structure of the universe and ancient civilizations. In the introduction, Socrates muses about the perfect society, described in Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> (c. 380 BC), and wonders if he and his guests might recollect a story which exemplifies such a society. Critias mentions a tale he considered to be historical, that would make the perfect example, and he then follows by describing Atlantis as is recorded in the <i>Critias</i>. In his account, ancient Athens seems to represent the "perfect society" and Atlantis its opponent, representing the very antithesis of the "perfect" traits described in the <i>Republic</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critias"><i>Critias</i></span></h3>
<p>According to Critias, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Hellenic</a> deities of old divided the land so that each deity might have their own lot; <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> was appropriately, and to his liking, bequeathed the island of Atlantis. The island was larger than <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Ancient Libya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> combined,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> but it was later sunk by an earthquake and became an impassable mud shoal, inhibiting travel to any part of the ocean. Plato asserted that the Egyptians described Atlantis as an island consisting mostly of mountains in the northern portions and along the shore and encompassing a great plain in an oblong shape in the south "extending in one direction three thousand <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stadia_(length)" title="Stadia (length)">stadia</a></i> [about 555 km; 345 mi], but across the center inland it was two thousand stadia [about 370 km; 230 mi]." Fifty stadia [9 km; 6 mi] from the coast was a mountain that was low on all sides ... broke it off all round about ... the central island itself was five stades in diameter [about 0.92 km; 0.57 mi].</p>
<p>In Plato's metaphorical tale, Poseidon fell in love with Cleito, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Evenor" title="Evenor">Evenor</a> and Leucippe, who bore him five pairs of male twins. The eldest of these, <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>, was made rightful king of the entire island and the ocean (called the Atlantic Ocean in his honor), and was given the mountain of his birth and the surrounding area as his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiefdom" title="Fiefdom">fiefdom</a>. Atlas's twin Gadeirus, or Eumelus in Greek, was given the extremity of the island toward the pillars of Hercules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The other four pairs of twins—Ampheres and Evaemon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mneseus" title="Mneseus">Mneseus</a> and Autochthon, Elasippus and Mestor, and Azaes and Diaprepes—were also given "rule over many men, and a large territory."</p>
<p>Poseidon carved the mountain where his love dwelt into a palace and enclosed it with three circular <a href="/wiki/Moat" title="Moat">moats</a> of increasing width, varying from one to three stadia and separated by rings of land proportional in size. The Atlanteans then built bridges northward from the mountain, making a route to the rest of the island. They dug a great canal to the sea, and alongside the bridges carved tunnels into the rings of rock so that ships could pass into the city around the mountain; they carved docks from the rock walls of the moats. Every passage to the city was guarded by gates and towers, and a wall surrounded each ring of the city. The walls were constructed of red, white, and black rock, quarried from the moats, and were covered with <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and the precious metal <a href="/wiki/Orichalcum" title="Orichalcum">orichalcum</a>, respectively.</p>
<p>According to Critias, 9,000 years before his lifetime a war took place between those outside the Pillars of Hercules at the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and those who dwelt within them. The Atlanteans had conquered the parts of Libya within the Pillars of Hercules, as far as Egypt, and the European continent as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrrhenia" title="Tyrrhenia">Tyrrhenia</a>, and had subjected its people to slavery. The Athenians led an alliance of resistors against the Atlantean empire, and as the alliance disintegrated, prevailed alone against the empire, liberating the occupied lands.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>But afterwards there occurred violent earthquakes and floods; and in a single day and night of misfortune all your warlike men in a body sank into the earth, and the island of Atlantis in like manner disappeared in the depths of the sea. For which reason the sea in those parts is impassable and impenetrable, because there is a shoal of mud in the way; and this was caused by the subsidence of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Logographer_(history)" title="Logographer (history)">logographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Hellanicus_of_Lesbos" title="Hellanicus of Lesbos">Hellanicus of Lesbos</a> wrote an earlier work entitled <i>Atlantis</i>, of which only a few fragments survive. Hellanicus' work appears to have been a genealogical one concerning the daughters of Atlas (Ἀτλαντὶς in Greek means "of Atlas"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luce_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luce-11">[11]</a></sup> but some authors have suggested a possible connection with Plato's island. <a href="/wiki/John_V._Luce" title="John V. Luce">John V. Luce</a> notes that when Plato writes about the genealogy of Atlantis's kings, he writes in the same style as Hellanicus, suggesting a similarity between a fragment of Hellanicus's work and an account in the <i>Critias</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luce_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Luce-11">[11]</a></sup> Rodney Castleden suggests that Plato may have borrowed his title from Hellanicus, who may have based his work on an earlier work about Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Castleden has pointed out that Plato wrote of Atlantis in 359 BC, when he returned to Athens from Sicily. He notes a number of parallels between the physical organisation and fortifications of <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a> and Plato's description of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Gunnar Rudberg was the first who elaborated upon the idea that Plato's attempt to realize his political ideas in the city of Syracuse could have heavily inspired the Atlantis account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretations">Interpretations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span></h3>
<p>Some ancient writers viewed Atlantis as fictional or metaphorical myth; others believed it to be real.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Crantor" title="Crantor">Crantor</a>, a student of Plato's student <a href="/wiki/Xenocrates" title="Xenocrates">Xenocrates</a>, is cited often as an example of a writer who thought the story to be historical fact. His work, a commentary on Plato's <i>Timaeus</i>, is lost, but <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoplatonist" title="Neoplatonist">Neoplatonist</a> of the fifth century AD, reports on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The passage in question has been represented in the modern literature either as claiming that Crantor visited Egypt, had conversations with priests, and saw hieroglyphs confirming the story, or, as claiming that he learned about them from other visitors to Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Proclus wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>As for the whole of this account of the Atlanteans, some say that it is unadorned history, such as Crantor, the first commentator on Plato. Crantor also says that Plato's contemporaries used to criticize him jokingly for not being the inventor of his Republic but copying the institutions of the Egyptians. Plato took these critics seriously enough to assign to the Egyptians this story about the Athenians and Atlanteans, so as to make them say that the Athenians really once lived according to that system.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The next sentence is often translated "Crantor adds, that this is testified by the prophets of the Egyptians, who assert that these particulars [which are narrated by Plato] are written on pillars which are still preserved." But in the original, the sentence starts not with the name Crantor but with the ambiguous <i>He</i>; whether this referred to Crantor or to Plato is the subject of considerable debate. Proponents of both Atlantis as a metaphorical myth and Atlantis as history have argued that the pronoun refers to Crantor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alan Cameron argues that the pronoun should be interpreted as referring to Plato, and that, when Proclus writes that "we must bear in mind concerning this whole feat of the Athenians, that it is neither a mere myth nor unadorned history, although some take it as history and others as myth", he is treating "Crantor's view as mere personal opinion, nothing more; in fact he first quotes and then dismisses it as representing one of the two unacceptable extremes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_1983_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_1983-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cameron also points out that whether <i>he</i> refers to Plato or to Crantor, the statement does not support conclusions such as Otto Muck's "Crantor came to Sais and saw there in the temple of <a href="/wiki/Neith" title="Neith">Neith</a> the column, completely covered with hieroglyphs, on which the history of Atlantis was recorded. Scholars translated it for him, and he testified that their account fully agreed with Plato's account of Atlantis"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> or J. V. Luce's suggestion that Crantor sent "a special enquiry to Egypt" and that he may simply be referring to Plato's own claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_1983_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron_1983-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another passage from the commentary by Proclus on the "Timaeus" gives a description of the geography of Atlantis:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>That an island of such nature and size once existed is evident from what is said by certain authors who investigated the things around the outer sea. For according to them, there were seven islands in that sea in their time, sacred to <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a>, and also three others of enormous size, one of which was sacred to Hades, another to Ammon, and another one between them to Poseidon, the extent of which was a thousand stadia [200 km]; and the inhabitants of it—they add—preserved the remembrance from their ancestors of the immeasurably large island of Atlantis which had really existed there and which for many ages had reigned over all islands in the Atlantic sea and which itself had like-wise been sacred to Poseidon. Now these things Marcellus has written in his <i>Aethiopica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Marcellus remains unidentified.</p>
<p>Other ancient historians and philosophers who believed in the existence of Atlantis were <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Some have theorized that, before the sixth century BC, the "Pillars of Hercules" may have applied to mountains on either side of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Laconia" title="Gulf of Laconia">Gulf of Laconia</a>, and also may have been part of the pillar cult of the Aegean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis.2C_J.L_1990_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis.2C_J.L_1990-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castledon.2C_Rodney_1998_p6_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castledon.2C_Rodney_1998_p6-36">[36]</a></sup> The mountains stood at either side of the southernmost gulf in Greece, the largest in the Peloponnese, and it opens onto the Mediterranean Sea. This would have placed Atlantis in the Mediterranean, lending credence to many details in Plato's discussion.</p>
<p>The fourth-century historian <a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a>, relying on a lost work by <a href="/wiki/Timagenes" title="Timagenes">Timagenes</a>, a historian writing in the first century BC, writes that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druids" title="Druids">Druids</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> said that part of the inhabitants of Gaul had migrated there from distant islands. Some have understood Ammianus's testimony as a claim that at the time of Atlantis's sinking into the sea, its inhabitants fled to western Europe; but Ammianus, in fact, says that "the Drasidae (Druids) recall that a part of the population is indigenous but others also migrated in from islands and lands beyond the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>" (<i>Res Gestae</i> 15.9), an indication that the immigrants came to Gaul from the north (Britain, the Netherlands, or Germany), not from a theorized location in the Atlantic Ocean to the south-west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Instead, the Celts who dwelled along the ocean were reported to venerate twin gods, (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscori" title="Dioscori">Dioscori</a>), who appeared to them coming from that ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_and_Christian">Jewish and Christian</span></h3>
<p>During the early first century AD, the Hellenistic Jewish philosopher <a href="/wiki/Philo" title="Philo">Philo</a> wrote about the destruction of Atlantis in his <i>On the Eternity of the World</i>, xxvi. 141, in a longer passage allegedly citing Aristotle's successor <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... And the island of Atalantes [translator's spelling; original: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἀτλαντίς</span>] which was greater than Africa and Asia, as Plato says in the Timaeus, in one day and night was overwhelmed beneath the sea in consequence of an extraordinary earthquake and inundation and suddenly disappeared, becoming sea, not indeed navigable, but full of gulfs and eddies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>There is the possibility that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clement_of_Rome" title="Clement of Rome">Clement of Rome</a> cryptically referred to Atlantis in his <a href="/wiki/First_Epistle_of_Clement" title="First Epistle of Clement">First Epistle of Clement</a>, 20: 8:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... The ocean which is impassable for men, and the worlds beyond it, are directed by the same ordinances of the Master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Barber_Lightfoot" title="Joseph Barber Lightfoot">Joseph Barber Lightfoot</a> (<i>Apostolic Fathers</i>, 1885, II, p. 84) noted on this passage: "Clement may possibly be referring to some known, but hardly accessible land, lying without the pillars of Hercules. But more probably he contemplated some unknown land in the far west beyond the ocean, like the fabled Atlantis of Plato ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other early Christian writers wrote about Atlantis, although they had mixed views on whether it once existed or was an untrustworthy myth of pagan origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> believed Atlantis was once real and wrote that in the Atlantic Ocean once existed "[the isle] that was equal in size to Libya or Asia"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> referring to Plato's geographical description of Atlantis. The early Christian apologist writer <a href="/wiki/Arnobius" title="Arnobius">Arnobius</a> also believed Atlantis once existed, but blamed its destruction on pagans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cosmas_Indicopleustes" title="Cosmas Indicopleustes">Cosmas Indicopleustes</a> in the sixth century wrote of Atlantis in his <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_Topography" title="Christian Topography">Christian Topography</a></i> in an attempt to prove his theory that the world was flat and surrounded by water:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Indicopleustes2010_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Indicopleustes2010-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... In like manner the philosopher Timaeus also describes this Earth as surrounded by the Ocean, and the Ocean as surrounded by the more remote earth. For he supposes that there is to westward an island, Atlantis, lying out in the Ocean, in the direction of Gadeira (Cadiz), of an enormous magnitude, and relates that the ten kings having procured mercenaries from the nations in this island came from the earth far away, and conquered Europe and Asia, but were afterwards conquered by the Athenians, while that island itself was submerged by God under the sea. Both Plato and Aristotle praise this philosopher, and Proclus has written a commentary on him. He himself expresses views similar to our own with some modifications, transferring the scene of the events from the east to the west. Moreover he mentions those ten generations as well as that earth which lies beyond the Ocean. And in a word it is evident that all of them borrow from Moses, and publish his statements as their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> treatise on computational <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> dated to AD 1378/79, alludes to the Atlantis myth in a discussion concerning the determination of zero points for the calculation of longitude:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (July 2012)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Some say that they [the inhabited regions] begin at the beginning of the western ocean [the Atlantic] and beyond. For in the earliest times [literally: the first days] there was an island in the middle of the ocean. There were scholars there, who isolated themselves in [the pursuit of] philosophy. In their day, that was the [beginning for measuring] the longitude[s] of the inhabited world. Today, it has become [covered by the?] sea, and it is ten degrees into the sea; and they reckon the beginning of longitude from the beginning of the western sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="595" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg/375px-Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg/563px-Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg 2x" width="375"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantis_map_1882_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map showing the supposed extent of the Atlantean Empire, from <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_L._Donnelly" title="Ignatius L. Donnelly">Ignatius L. Donnelly</a>'s <i>Atlantis: the Antediluvian World</i>, 1882 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<p>Aside from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s original account, modern interpretations regarding Atlantis are an amalgamation of diverse, speculative movements that began in the sixteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Contemporary perceptions of Atlantis share roots with <a href="/wiki/Mayanism" title="Mayanism">Mayanism</a>, which can be traced to the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Age" title="Modern Age">Modern Age</a>, when European imaginations were fueled by their initial encounters with the indigenous peoples of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoopes_2011_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoopes_2011-51">[51]</a></sup> From this era sprang <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian" title="Utopian">utopian</a> visions that would inspire many subsequent generations of theorists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoopes_2011_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoopes_2011-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of these interpretations are considered <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">pseudohistory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscience</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pseudoarchaeology" title="Pseudoarchaeology">pseudoarchaeology</a>, as they have presented their works as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic" title="Academic">academic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific">scientific</a>, but lack the standards or criteria.</p>
<p>The Flemish cartographer and geographer <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> is believed to have been the first person to imagine that the continents were joined together before <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">drifting</a> to their present positions. In the 1596 edition of his <i>Thesaurus Geographicus</i> he wrote: "Unless it be a fable, the island of Gadir or Gades [<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz" title="Cadiz">Cadiz</a>] will be the remaining part of the island of Atlantis or America, which was not sunk (as Plato reports in the <i>Timaeus</i>) so much as torn away from Europe and Africa by earthquakes and flood... The traces of the ruptures are shown by the projections of Europe and Africa and the indentations of America in the parts of the coasts of these three said lands that face each other to anyone who, using a map of the world, carefully considered them. So that anyone may say with <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> in Book 2, that what Plato says of the island of Atlantis on the authority of Solon is not a figment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantis_pseudohistory">Atlantis pseudohistory</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_influential_literature">Early influential literature</span></h5>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopia</a>" (from "no place") was coined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_More" title="Sir Thomas More">Sir Thomas More</a> in his sixteenth-century work of <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Utopia_(book)" title="Utopia (book)">Utopia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup> Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s Atlantis and travelers' accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, More described an imaginary land set in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> His idealistic vision established a connection between the Americas and utopian societies, a theme that was further solidified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Francis_Bacon" title="Sir Francis Bacon">Sir Francis Bacon</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">The New Atlantis</a></i> (c. 1623).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoopes_2011_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hoopes_2011-51">[51]</a></sup> A character in the narrative gives a history of Atlantis that is similar to Plato's and places Atlantis in America. People had begun believing that the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> ruins could possibly be the remnants of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_Mayanism">Impact of Mayanism</span></h5>
<p>Much speculation began as to the origins of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>, which led to a variety of narratives and publications that tried to rationalize the discoveries within the context of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and that had undertones of <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> in their connections between the Old and New World. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">Europeans</a> believed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_people" title="Indigenous people">indigenous people</a> to be inferior and incapable of building that which was now in ruins and by sharing a common history, they insinuate that another race must have been responsible.</p>
<p>In the middle and late nineteenth century, several renowned <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> scholars, starting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Etienne_Brasseur_de_Bourbourg" title="Charles Etienne Brasseur de Bourbourg">Charles Etienne Brasseur de Bourbourg</a>, and including <a href="/wiki/Edward_Herbert_Thompson" title="Edward Herbert Thompson">Edward Herbert Thompson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Le_Plongeon" title="Augustus Le Plongeon">Augustus Le Plongeon</a>, formally proposed that Atlantis was somehow related to Mayan and <a href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> culture.</p>
<p>The French scholar Brasseur de Bourbourg traveled extensively through Mesoamerica in the mid-1800s, and was renowned for his translations of <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan</a> texts, most notably the sacred book <a href="/wiki/Popol_Vuh" title="Popol Vuh">Popol Vuh</a>, as well as a comprehensive history of the region. Soon after these publications, however, Brasseur de Bourbourg lost his academic credibility, due to his claim that the <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya peoples</a> had descended from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toltecs" title="Toltecs">Toltecs</a>, people he believed were the surviving population of the racially superior civilization of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> His work combined with the skillful, romantic illustrations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Frederic_Waldeck" title="Jean Frederic Waldeck">Jean Frederic Waldeck</a>, which visually alluded to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and other aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, created an authoritative <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> that excited much interest in the connections between worlds.</p>
<p>Inspired by Brasseur de Bourbourg's diffusion theories, the pseudoarchaeologist Augustus Le Plongeon traveled to Mesoamerica and performed some of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excavations" title="Excavations">excavations</a> of many famous Mayan ruins. Le Plongeon invented narratives, such as the kingdom of Moo saga, which romantically drew connections to him, his wife Alice, and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">Egyptian</a> deities <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>, as well as to <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a>, who had just discovered the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> from <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poetry</a> (that had been described as merely mythical).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> He also believed that he had found connections between the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan languages</a>, which produced a <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> of the destruction of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Ignatius_Donnelly">Ignatius Donnelly</span></h5>
<p>The 1882 publication of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantis:_the_Antediluvian_World" title="Atlantis: the Antediluvian World">Atlantis: the Antediluvian World</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_L._Donnelly" title="Ignatius L. Donnelly">Ignatius L. Donnelly</a> stimulated much popular interest in Atlantis. He was greatly inspired by early works in <a href="/wiki/Mayanism" title="Mayanism">Mayanism</a>, and like them, attempted to establish that all known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_civilizations" title="Ancient civilizations">ancient civilizations</a> were descended from Atlantis, which he saw as a technologically sophisticated, more advanced <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>. Donnelly drew parallels between creation stories in the Old and New Worlds, attributing the connections to Atlantis, where he believed the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a> existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> As implied by the title of his book, he also believed that Atlantis was destroyed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Flood" title="Great Flood">Great Flood</a> mentioned in the Bible.</p>
<p>Donnelly is credited as the "father of the nineteenth century Atlantis revival" and is the reason the <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a> endures today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> He unintentionally promoted an alternative method of inquiry to history and science, and the idea that myths contain hidden information that opens them to "ingenious" interpretation by people who believe they have new or special insight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Madame_Blavatsky_and_the_Theosophists">Madame Blavatsky and the Theosophists</span></h5>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Atlantis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="623" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Map_of_Atlantis.jpg/350px-Map_of_Atlantis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Map_of_Atlantis.jpg/525px-Map_of_Atlantis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Map_of_Atlantis.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Atlantis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Map of Atlantis according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Scott-Elliott" title="William Scott-Elliott">William Scott-Elliott</a> (<i>The Story of Atlantis</i>, Russian edition, 1910)</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Russian mystic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helena_Petrovna_Blavatsky" title="Helena Petrovna Blavatsky">Helena Petrovna Blavatsky</a> and her partner <a href="/wiki/Henry_Steel_Olcott" title="Henry Steel Olcott">Henry Steel Olcott</a> founded their <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> in the 1870s with a philosophy that combined western <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romanticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">eastern</a> religious concepts. Blavatsky and her followers in this group are often cited as the founders of <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> and other spiritual movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Blavatsky took up <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donnelly" title="Donnelly">Donnelly</a>'s interpretations when she wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Doctrine" title="The Secret Doctrine">The Secret Doctrine</a></i> (1888), which she claimed was originally dictated in Atlantis. She maintained that the Atlanteans were cultural heroes (contrary to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, who describes them mainly as a military threat). She believed in a form of racial <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> (as opposed to primate evolution), in which the Atlanteans were the fourth "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_Race" title="Root Race">Root Race</a>", succeeded by the fifth and most superior "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>" (her own race).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theosophists" title="Theosophists">Theosophists</a> believed that the civilization of Atlantis reached its peak between 1,000,000 and 900,000 years ago, but destroyed itself through internal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> brought about by the dangerous use of <a href="/wiki/Psychic" title="Psychic">psychic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> powers of the inhabitants. <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophy" title="Anthroposophy">anthroposophy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waldorf_Schools" title="Waldorf Schools">Waldorf Schools</a>, along with other well known Theosophists, such as <a href="/wiki/Annie_Besant" title="Annie Besant">Annie Besant</a>, also wrote of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural" title="Cultural">cultural</a> evolution in much the same vein.</p>
<p>Some subsequent occultists have followed Blavatsky, at least to the point of tracing the lineage of occult practices back to Atlantis. Among the most famous is <a href="/wiki/Dion_Fortune" title="Dion Fortune">Dion Fortune</a> in her <i>Esoteric Orders and Their Work</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazism_and_occultism">Nazism and occultism</span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazism_and_occultism" title="Nazism and occultism">Nazism and occultism</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blavatsky" title="Blavatsky">Blavatsky</a> was also inspired by the work of the eighteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Sylvain_Bailly" title="Jean-Sylvain Bailly">Jean-Sylvain Bailly</a>, who had "Orientalized" the Atlantis <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myth</a> in his mythical continent of <a href="/wiki/Hyperborea" title="Hyperborea">Hyperborea</a>, a reference to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_myths" title="Greek myths">Greek myths</a> featuring a Northern European region of the same name, home to a giant, godlike race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edelstein_2006:_268_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edelstein_2006:_268-62">[62]</a></sup> Her reshaping of this theory in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Doctrine" title="The Secret Doctrine">The Secret Doctrine</a></i> provided the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> with a mythological precedent and a pretext for their ideological platform and <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">their subsequent genocide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edelstein_2006:_268_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edelstein_2006:_268-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a>'s writing in 1934 also suggested that the Atlanteans were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperborean" title="Hyperborean">Hyperborean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> supermen who originated at the North Pole (see <a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Thule</a>). Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a> (in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Myth_of_the_Twentieth_Century" title="The Myth of the Twentieth Century">The Myth of the Twentieth Century</a></i>, 1930) spoke of a "Nordic-Atlantean" or "Aryan-Nordic" master race.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Edgar_Cayce">Edgar Cayce</span></h5>
<p><a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cayce" title="Edgar Cayce">Edgar Cayce</a> was a man from humble upbringings in <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> who allegedly possessed <a href="/wiki/Psychic" title="Psychic">psychic</a> abilities, which were performed from a trance-like state. In addition to allegedly healing the sick from this state, he also spoke frequently on the topic of Atlantis. In his "life readings," he purportedly revealed that many of his subjects were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reincarnations" title="Reincarnations">reincarnations</a> of people who had lived on Atlantis. By tapping into their <a href="/wiki/Collective_consciousness" title="Collective consciousness">collective consciousness</a>, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akashic_Records" title="Akashic Records">Akashic Records</a>" (a term borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Theosophy" title="Theosophy">Theosophy</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> he declared that he was able to give detailed descriptions of the lost continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> He also asserted that Atlantis would "rise" again in the 1960s (sparking much popularity of the myth in that decade) and that there is a "<a href="/wiki/Hall_of_Records" title="Hall of Records">Hall of Records</a>" beneath the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Sphinx" title="Egyptian Sphinx">Egyptian Sphinx</a>, which holds the historical texts of Atlantis.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_times">Recent times</span></h4>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a> became widely accepted during the 1960s, and the increased understanding of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> demonstrated the impossibility of a lost continent in the geologically recent past,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> most "Lost Continent" theories of Atlantis began to wane in popularity.</p>
<p>Plato scholar <a href="/wiki/Julia_Annas" title="Julia Annas">Julia Annas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regents_Professor" title="Regents Professor">Regents Professor</a> of Philosophy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Arizona" title="University of Arizona">University of Arizona</a>, had this to say on the matter:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The continuing industry of discovering Atlantis illustrates the dangers of reading Plato. For he is clearly using what has become a standard device of fiction—stressing the historicity of an event (and the discovery of hitherto unknown authorities) as an indication that what follows is fiction. <i>The idea is that we should use the story to examine our ideas of government and power</i>. We have missed the point if instead of thinking about these issues we go off exploring the sea bed. The continuing misunderstanding of Plato as historian here enables us to see why his distrust of imaginative writing is sometimes justified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>One of the proposed explanations for the historical context of the Atlantis story is a warning of Plato to his contemporary fourth-century fellow-citizens against their striving for naval power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Feder" title="Kenneth Feder">Kenneth Feder</a> points out that Critias's story in the <i>Timaeus</i> provides a major clue. In the dialogue, Critias says, referring to Socrates' hypothetical society:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>And when you were speaking yesterday about your city and citizens, the tale which I have just been repeating to you came into my mind, and I remarked with astonishment how, by some mysterious coincidence, you agreed in almost every particular with the narrative of Solon. ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Feder quotes A. E. Taylor, who wrote, "We could not be told much more plainly that the whole narrative of Solon's conversation with the priests and his intention of writing the poem about Atlantis are an invention of Plato's fancy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_hypotheses">Location hypotheses</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Location_hypotheses_of_Atlantis" title="Location hypotheses of Atlantis">Location hypotheses of Atlantis</a></div>
<p>Since Donnelly's day, there have been dozens of locations proposed for Atlantis, to the point where the name has become a generic concept, divorced from the specifics of Plato's account. This is reflected in the fact that many proposed sites are not within the Atlantic at all. Few today are scholarly or archaeological hypotheses, while others have been made by <a href="/wiki/Psychic" title="Psychic">psychic</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Cayce" title="Edgar Cayce">Edgar Cayce</a>) or other <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a> means. (The Atlantis researchers Jacques Collina-Girard and Georgeos Díaz-Montexano, for instance, each claim the other's hypothesis is pseudoscience.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Many of the proposed sites share some of the characteristics of the Atlantis story (water, catastrophic end, relevant time period), but none has been demonstrated to be a true historical Atlantis.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santorini_Landsat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="1191" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Santorini_Landsat.jpg/250px-Santorini_Landsat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Santorini_Landsat.jpg/375px-Santorini_Landsat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Santorini_Landsat.jpg/500px-Santorini_Landsat.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santorini_Landsat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite image of the islands of <a href="/wiki/Santorini" title="Santorini">Santorini</a>. From the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_eruption" title="Minoan eruption">Minoan eruption</a> event, and the 1964 discovery of <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_(Santorini)" title="Akrotiri (Santorini)">Akrotiri</a> on the island, this location is one of many sites purported to have been the location of Atlantis</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_or_near_the_Mediterranean_Sea">In or near the Mediterranean Sea</span></h3>
<p>Most of the historically proposed locations are in or near the Mediterranean Sea: islands such as <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santorini" title="Santorini">Santorini</a> (Thera), Sicily, Cyprus, and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>; land-based cities or states such as <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tartessos" title="Tartessos">Tartessos</a>, and Tantalis (in the province of <a href="/wiki/Manisa" title="Manisa">Manisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai_peninsula" title="Sinai peninsula">Sinai</a> or <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and northwestern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera_eruption" title="Thera eruption">Thera eruption</a>, dated to the seventeenth or sixteenth century BC, caused a large <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> that some experts hypothesize devastated the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> on the nearby island of Crete, further leading some to believe that this may have been the catastrophe that inspired the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>A. G. Galanopoulos argued that Plato's dating of 9,000 years before <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a>'s time was the result of an error in translation, probably from Egyptian into Greek, which produced "thousands" instead of "hundreds". Such an error would also rescale Plato's Atlantis to the size of Crete, while leaving the city the size of the crater on Thera; 900 years before Solon would be the fifteenth century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In the area of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> the following locations have been proposed: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ancomah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ancomah (page does not exist)">Ancomah</a> (a legendary place near <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trabzon</a>).</p>
<p>Others have noted that, before the sixth century BC, the mountains on either side of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Laconia" title="Gulf of Laconia">Gulf of Laconia</a> were called the "Pillars of Hercules",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis.2C_J.L_1990_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis.2C_J.L_1990-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castledon.2C_Rodney_1998_p6_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castledon.2C_Rodney_1998_p6-36">[36]</a></sup> and they could be the geographical location being described in ancient reports upon which Plato was basing his story. The mountains stood at either side of the southernmost gulf in Greece, the largest in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>, and that gulf opens onto the Mediterranean Sea. If from the beginning of discussions, misinterpretation of Gibraltar as the location rather than being at the Gulf of Laconia, would lend itself to many erroneous concepts regarding the location of Atlantis. Plato may have not been aware of the difference. The Laconian pillars open to the south toward Crete and beyond which is Egypt. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thera_eruption" title="Thera eruption">Thera eruption</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Late Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze Age collapse</a> affected that area and might have been the devastation to which the sources used by Plato referred. Significant events such as these would have been likely material for tales passed from one generation to another for almost a thousand years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Atlantic_Ocean">In the Atlantic Ocean</span></h3>
<p>The location of Atlantis in the Atlantic Ocean has a certain appeal given the closely related names. Popular culture often places Atlantis there, perpetuating the original Platonic setting as they understand it. The <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madeira_Islands" title="Madeira Islands">Madeira Islands</a> have been identified as a possible location,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canarias_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canarias-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madeira-Azores_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madeira-Azores-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vidal-Naquet_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vidal-Naquet-82">[82]</a></sup> west of the Straits of Gibraltar, but in relative proximity to the Mediterranean Sea. Detailed studies of their geomorphology and geology have demonstrated, however, that they have been steadily uplifted, without any significant periods of subsidence, over the last four million years, by geologic processes such as erosional unloading, gravitational unloading, lithospheric flexure induced by adjacent islands, and volcanic underplating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MenendezOthers2009_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-MenendezOthers2009-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MecoOthers2007_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MecoOthers2007-84">[84]</a></sup> Various islands or island groups in the Atlantic were also identified as possible locations, notably the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madeira-Azores_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Madeira-Azores-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vidal-Naquet_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vidal-Naquet-82">[82]</a></sup> Similarly, cores of sediment covering the ocean bottom surrounding the Azores and other evidence demonstrate that it has been an undersea plateau for millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuangOther1979_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuangOther1979-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DennielouOther1999_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-DennielouOther1999-86">[86]</a></sup> The submerged island of <a href="/wiki/Spartel" title="Spartel">Spartel</a> near the Strait of Gibraltar has also been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antiquity_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Antiquity-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Europe">In Europe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doggerland.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="545" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/250px-Doggerland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/375px-Doggerland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/500px-Doggerland.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doggerland.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing hypothetical extent of <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a> (c. 8,000 BC), which provided a land bridge between Great Britain and continental Europe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several hypotheses place the sunken island in northern Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olof_Rudbeck" title="Olof Rudbeck">Olof Rudbeck</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olof_Rudbeck" title="Olof Rudbeck">Atland</a></i>, 1672–1702). Doggerland, as well as <a href="/wiki/Viking_Bergen_Island" title="Viking Bergen Island">Viking Bergen Island</a>, is thought to have been flooded by a <a href="/wiki/Megatsunami" title="Megatsunami">megatsunami</a> following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storegga_slide" title="Storegga slide">Storegga slide</a> of c. 6100 BC. Some have proposed the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea#Seabed" title="Celtic Sea">Celtic Shelf</a> as a possible location, and that there is a link to Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, a team, working on a documentary for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Channel" title="National Geographic Channel">National Geographic Channel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Geographic_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Geographic-89">[89]</a></sup> led by Professor Richard Freund from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hartford" title="University of Hartford">University of Hartford</a>, claimed to have found possible evidence of Atlantis in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The team identified its possible location within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marshlands" title="Marshlands">marshlands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Do%C3%B1ana_National_Park" title="Doñana National Park">Doñana National Park</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Las_Marismas" title="Las Marismas">the area</a> that once was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lacus_Ligustinus" title="Lacus Ligustinus">Lacus Ligustinus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huelva_Province" title="Huelva Province">Huelva</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz_Province" title="Cadiz Province">Cádiz</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seville_province" title="Seville province">Seville provinces</a>, and they speculated that Atlantis had been destroyed by a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> extrapolating results from a previous study by Spanish researchers, published four years earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spanish scientists have dismissed Freund's speculations, claiming that he sensationalised their work. The anthropologist Juan Villarías-Robles, who works with the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_National_Research_Council" title="Spanish National Research Council">Spanish National Research Council</a>, said, "Richard Freund was a newcomer to our project and appeared to be involved in his own very controversial issue concerning King Solomon's search for ivory and gold in <a href="/wiki/Tartessos" title="Tartessos">Tartessos</a>, the well documented settlement in the Doñana area established in the first millennium BC", and described Freund's claims as "fanciful".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>A similar theory had previously been put forward by a German researcher, Rainer W. Kühne, that is based only on satellite imagery and places Atlantis in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marismas_de_Hinojos" title="Marismas de Hinojos">Marismas de Hinojos</a>, north of the city of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Antiquity_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Antiquity-87">[87]</a></sup> Before that, the historian <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Schulten" title="Adolf Schulten">Adolf Schulten</a> had stated in the 1920s that Plato had used Tartessos as the basis for his Atlantis myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_locations">Other locations</span></h3>
<p>Several writers have speculated that <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> is the site of Atlantis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> while others have proposed <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a> locations such as the alleged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_sunken_city" title="Cuban sunken city">Cuban sunken city</a> off the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guanahacabibes_peninsula" title="Guanahacabibes peninsula">Guanahacabibes peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bahamas" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Bermuda_Triangle" title="Bermuda Triangle">Bermuda Triangle</a>. Areas in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> and Indian Oceans have also been proposed including <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Sundaland" title="Sundaland">Sundaland</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Likewise some have speculated that the continent of South America bears striking similarities to the description of Atlantis by Plato, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano" title="Altiplano">Altiplano</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>. The stories of a lost continent off the coast of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, named "<a href="/wiki/Kumari_Kandam" title="Kumari Kandam">Kumari Kandam</a>," have inspired some to draw parallels to Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_interpretations">Literary interpretations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_versions">Ancient versions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1551" data-file-width="681" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png/180px-POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png/270px-POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png/360px-POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:POxy1084_Hellanicus_Atlantis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A fragment of <i>Atlantis</i> by Hellanicus of Lesbos</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In order to give his account of Atlantis <a href="/wiki/Verisimilitude_(fiction)" title="Verisimilitude (fiction)">verisimilitude</a>, Plato mentions that the story was heard by <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a> in Egypt, and transmitted orally over several generations through the family of Dropides, until it reached Critias, a dialogue speaker in <i>Timaeus</i> and <i>Critias</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Solon had supposedly tried to adapt the Atlantis <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a> into a poem (that if published, was to be greater than the works of <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>). While it was never completed, Solon passed on the story to Dropides. Modern classicists deny the existence of Solon's Atlantis poem and the story as an oral tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Instead, Plato is thought to be the sole inventor or fabricator. <a href="/wiki/Hellanicus_of_Lesbos" title="Hellanicus of Lesbos">Hellanicus of Lesbos</a> used the word Atlantis as the title for a poem published before Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> a fragment of which may be <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a> Papyrus 11, 1359.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> This work only describes the Atlantides (the daughters of Atlas), however, and has no relation to Plato's Atlantis account.</p>
<p>In the new era, the third century AD <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonist</a> Zoticus wrote an epic poem based on Plato's account of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Plato's work may already have inspired <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parodic</a> imitation, however. Writing only a few decades after the <i>Timaeus</i> and <i>Critias</i>, the historian <a href="/wiki/Theopompus" title="Theopompus">Theopompus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a> wrote of a land beyond the ocean known as <a href="/wiki/Meropis" title="Meropis">Meropis</a>. This description was included in Book 8 of his <i>Philippica</i>, which contains a dialogue between <a href="/wiki/Silenus" title="Silenus">Silenus</a> and King <a href="/wiki/Midas" title="Midas">Midas</a>. Silenus describes the Meropids, a race of men who grow to twice normal size, and inhabit two cities on the island of Meropis: <i>Eusebes</i> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Εὐσεβής</span>, "Pious-town") and <i>Machimos</i> (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Μάχιμος</span>, "Fighting-town"). He also reports that an army of ten million soldiers crossed the ocean to conquer <a href="/wiki/Hyperborea" title="Hyperborea">Hyperborea</a>, but abandoned this proposal when they realized that the Hyperboreans were the luckiest people on earth. Heinz-Günther Nesselrath has argued that these and other details of Silenus' story are meant as imitation and exaggeration of the Atlantis story, by parody, for the purpose of exposing Plato's ideas to ridicule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utopias_and_dystopias">Utopias and dystopias</span></h3>
<p>The creation of <a href="/wiki/Utopian_and_dystopian_fiction" title="Utopian and dystopian fiction">Utopian and dystopian fictions</a> was renewed after the Renaissance, most notably in Francis Bacon’s <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i> (1627), the description of an ideal society that he located off the western coast of America. Thomas Heyrick (1649-1694) followed him with “The New Atlantis” (1687), a satirical poem in three parts. His new continent of uncertain location, perhaps even a floating island either in the sea or the sky, serves as background for his exposure of what he described in a second edition as “A True Character of Popery and Jesuitism”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>The title of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Atalantis" title="The New Atalantis">The New Atalantis</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Delarivier_Manley" title="Delarivier Manley">Delarivier Manley</a> (1709), distinguished from the two others by the single letter, is an equally dystopian work but set this time on a fictional Mediterranean island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In it sexual violence and exploitation is made a metaphor for the hypocritical behaviour of politicians in their dealings with the general public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In Manley’s case, the target of satire was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(UK)" title="Whig Party (UK)">Whig Party</a>, while in David Maclean Parry's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scarlet_Empire" title="The Scarlet Empire">The Scarlet Empire</a></i> (1906) it is <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">Socialism</a> as practised in foundered Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> It was followed in Russia by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velemir_Khlebnikov" title="Velemir Khlebnikov">Velemir Khlebnikov</a>'s poem <i>The Fall of Atlantis</i> (<i>Gibel' Atlantidy</i>, 1912), which is set in a future rationalist dystopia that has discovered the secret of immortality and is so dedicated to progress that it has lost touch with the past. When the high priest of this ideology is tempted by a slave girl into an act of irrationality, he murders her and precipitates a second flood, above which her severed head floats vengefully among the stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>A slightly later work, <i>The Ancient of Atlantis</i> (Boston, 1915) by Albert Armstrong Manship, expounds the Atlantean wisdom that is to redeem the earth. Its three parts consist of a verse narrative of the life and training of an Atlantean wise one, followed by his Utopian moral teachings and then a psychic drama set in modern times in which a reincarnated child embodying the lost wisdom is reborn on earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> eyes, Atlantis had a more intimate interpretation. The land had been a colonial power which, although it had brought civilization to ancient Europe, had also enslaved its peoples. Its tyrannical fall from grace had contributed to the fate that had overtaken it, but now its disappearance had unbalanced the world. This was the point of view of <a href="/wiki/Jacint_Verdaguer" title="Jacint Verdaguer">Jacint Verdaguer</a>’s vast mythological epic <i>L’Atlantida</i> (1877). After the sinking of the former continent, Hercules travels east across the Atlantic to found the city of <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> and then departs westward again to the <a href="/wiki/Hesperides" title="Hesperides">Hesperides</a>. The story is told by a hermit to a shipwrecked mariner, who is inspired to follow in his tracks and so “call the New World into existence to redress the balance of the Old”. This mariner, of course, was <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Verdaguer’s poem was written in <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>, but was widely translated in both Europe and Hispano-America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> One response was the similarly entitled Argentinian <i>Atlantida</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olegario_Victor_Andrade" title="Olegario Victor Andrade">Olegario Victor Andrade</a> (1881), which sees in “Enchanted Atlantis that Plato foresaw, a golden promise to the fruitful race” of Latins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The bad example of the colonising world remains, however. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jose_Juan_Tablada" title="Jose Juan Tablada">Jose Juan Tablada</a> characterises its threat in his “De Atlántida” (1894) through the beguiling picture of the lost world populated by the underwater creatures of Classical myth, among whom is the <a href="/wiki/Siren_(mythology)" title="Siren (mythology)">Siren</a> of its final stanza with</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>her eye on the keel of the wandering vessel</dd>
<dd>that in passing deflowers the sea’s smooth mirror,</dd>
<dd>launching into the night her amorous warbling</dd>
<dd>and the dulcet lullaby of her treacherous voice!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>There is a similar ambivalence in <a href="/wiki/Janus_Djurhuus" title="Janus Djurhuus">Janus Djurhuus</a>’ six-stanza “Atlantis” (1917), where a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language_conflict" title="Faroese language conflict">Faroese linguistic revival</a> grants it an ancient pedigree by linking Greek to Norse legend. In the poem a female figure rising from the sea against a background of Classical palaces is recognised as a priestess of Atlantis.
