The poet recalls “that the Faroes lie there in the north Atlantic Ocean/ where before lay the poet-dreamt lands,” but also that in Norse belief, such a figure only appears to those about to drown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_land_lost_in_the_distance">A land lost in the distance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="360" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg/220px-Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg/330px-Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_493_Djurhuus_poems_-_atlantis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Faroe Islands postage stamp honoring <a href="/wiki/Janus_Djurhuus" title="Janus Djurhuus">Janus Djurhuus</a>' "Atlantis"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The fact that Atlantis is a lost land has made of it a metaphor for something no longer attainable. For the American poet Edith Willis Linn Forbes (1865-1945), “The Lost Atlantis” stands for idealisation of the past; the present moment can only be treasured once that is realised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ella_Wheeler_Wilcox" title="Ella Wheeler Wilcox">Ella Wheeler Wilcox</a> finds the location of “The Lost Land” (1910) in one’s carefree youthful past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Similarly, for the Irish poet <a href="/wiki/Eavan_Boland" title="Eavan Boland">Eavan Boland</a> in “Atlantis, a lost sonnet” (2007), the idea was defined when “the old fable-makers searched hard for a word/ to convey that what is gone is gone forever”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>For some male poets too, the idea of Atlantis is constructed from what cannot be obtained. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bewley" title="Charles Bewley">Charles Bewley</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Newdigate_Prize" title="Newdigate Prize">Newdigate Prize</a> poem (1910) thinks it grows from dissatisfaction with one’s condition,</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>And, because life is partly sweet</dd>
<dd>And ever girt about with pain,</dd>
<dd>We take the sweetness, and are fain</dd>
<dd>To set it free from grief's alloy</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>in a dream of Atlantis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Similarly for the Australian <a href="/wiki/Gary_Catalano" title="Gary Catalano">Gary Catalano</a> in a 1982 prose poem, it is “a vision that sank under the weight of its own perfection”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a>, however, suggests a way out of such frustration through the metaphor of journeying toward Atlantis in his poem of 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> While travelling, he advises the one setting out, you will meet with many definitions of the goal in view, only realising at the end that the way has all the time led inward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epic_narratives">Epic narratives</span></h3>
<p>A few late nineteenth century verse narratives complement the <a href="/wiki/Atlantis_in_popular_culture#Fiction" title="Atlantis in popular culture">genre fiction</a> that was beginning to be written at the same period. Two of them report the disaster that overtook the continent as related by long-lived survivors. In <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Tennyson" title="Frederick Tennyson">Frederick Tennyson</a>’s <i>Atlantis</i> (1888) an ancient Greek mariner sails west and discovers an inhabited island, which is all that remains of the former kingdom. He learns of its end and views the shattered remnant of its former glory, from which a few had escaped to set up the Mediterranean civilisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In the second, <i>Mona, Queen of Lost Atlantis: An Idyllic Re-embodiment of Long Forgotten History</i> (Los Angeles CA 1925) by James Logue Dryden (1840-1925), the story is told in a series of visions. A Seer is taken to Mona’s burial chamber in the ruins of Atlantis, where she revives and describes the catastrophe. There follows a survey of the lost civilisations of Hyperborea and Lemuria as well as Atlantis, accompanied by much spiritualist lore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>William Walton Hoskins (1856-1919) admits to the readers of his <i>Atlantis and other poems</i> (Cleveland OH, 1881), that he is only 24. Its melodramatic plot concerns the poisoning of the descendent of god-born kings. The usurping poisoner is poisoned in his turn, following which the continent is swallowed in the waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Asian gods people the landscape of <i>The Lost Island</i> (Ottawa 1889) by Edward Taylor Fletcher (1816–97). An angel foresees impending catastrophe and that the people will be allowed to escape if their semi-divine rulers will sacrifice themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> A final example, Edward N. Beecher’s <i>The Lost Atlantis or The Great Deluge of All</i> (Cleveland OH, 1898) is just a doggerel vehicle for its author’s opinions: that the continent was the location of the Garden of Eden; that Darwin’s theory of evolution is correct, as are Donnelly’s views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Atlantis was to become a theme in Russia following the 1890s, taken up in unfinished poems by <a href="/wiki/Valery_Bryusov" title="Valery Bryusov">Valery Bryusov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Balmont" title="Konstantin Balmont">Konstantin Balmont</a>, as well as in a drama by the schoolgirl <a href="/wiki/Larisa_Reisner" title="Larisa Reisner">Larisa Reisner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> One other long narrative poem was published in New York by George V. Golokhvastoff. His 250-page <i>The Fall of Atlantis</i> (1938) records how a high priest, distressed by the prevailing degeneracy of the ruling classes, seeks to create an androgynous being from royal twins as a means to overcome this polarity. When he is unable to control the forces unleashed by his occult ceremony, the continent is destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_representations">Artistic representations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<p>The Spanish composer <a href="/wiki/Manuel_de_Falla" title="Manuel de Falla">Manuel de Falla</a> worked on a dramatic cantata based on Verdaguer’s <i>L’Atlántida</i>, during the last 20 years of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The name has been affixed to symphonies by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janis_Ivanovs" title="Janis Ivanovs">Janis Ivanovs</a> (1941),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Richard Nanes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> and Vaclav Buzek (2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> There was also the symphonic celebration of <a href="/wiki/Alan_Hovhaness" title="Alan Hovhaness">Alan Hovhaness</a>: "Fanfare for the New Atlantis" (Op. 281, 1975).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Bohemian-American composer and arranger <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Frank_Safranek" title="Vincent Frank Safranek">Vincent Frank Safranek</a> wrote <i>Atlantis (The Lost Continent) Suite in Four Parts</i>; I. Nocturne and Morning Hymn of Praise, II. A Court Function, III. ″I Love Thee″ (The Prince and Aana), IV. The Destruction of Atlantis, for military (concert) band in 1913.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting_and_sculpture">Painting and sculpture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_(1908).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1112" data-file-width="1200" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg/220px-Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg/330px-Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg/440px-Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_%281908%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Terror_Antiquus_by_L.Bakst_(1908).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leon Bakst’s vision of cosmic catastrophe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Paintings of the submersion of Atlantis are comparatively rare. In the seventeenth century there was <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_de_Nom%C3%A9" title="François de Nomé">François de Nomé</a>’s “The Fall of Atlantis”, which shows a tidal wave surging toward a Baroque city frontage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The style of architecture apart, it is not very different from <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Roerich" title="Nicholas Roerich">Nicholas Roerich</a>’s “The Last of Atlantis” of 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most dramatic depiction of the catastrophe was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leon_Bakst" title="Leon Bakst">Leon Bakst</a>’s “Ancient Terror” (<i>Terror Antiquus</i>, 1908), although it does not name Atlantis directly. It is a mountain-top view of a rocky bay breached by the sea, which is washing inland about the tall structures of an ancient city. A streak of lightning crosses the upper half of the painting, while below it rises the impassive figure of an enigmatic goddess who holds a blue dove between her breasts. <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Ivanov_(poet)" title="Vyacheslav Ivanov (poet)">Vyacheslav Ivanov</a> identified the subject as Atlantis in a public lecture on the painting given in 1909, the year it was first exhibited, and he has been followed by other commentators in the years since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sculptures referencing Atlantis have often been stylized single figures. One of the earliest was <a href="/wiki/Einar_J%C3%B3nsson" title="Einar Jónsson">Einar Jónsson</a>’s <i>The King of Atlantis</i> (1919–1922), now in the garden of his museum in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reykjavik" title="Reykjavik">Reykjavik</a>. It represents a single figure, clad in a belted skirt and wearing a large triangular helmet, who sits on an ornate throne supported between two young bulls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The walking female entitled <i>Atlantis</i> (1946) by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Me%C5%A1trovi%C4%87" title="Ivan Meštrović">Ivan Meštrović</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> was from a series inspired by ancient Greek figures <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> with the symbolical meaning of unjustified suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the case of the <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> fountain feature known as <i>The Man of Atlantis</i> (2003) by the Belgian sculptor <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Luk_van_Soom&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Luk van Soom (page does not exist)">Luk van Soom</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luk_van_Soom" title="nl:Luk van Soom">nl</a>)</span>, the 4-metre tall figure wearing a diving suit steps from a plinth into the spray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> It looks light-hearted but the artist’s comment on it makes a serious point: "Because habitable land will be scarce, it is no longer improbable that we will return to the water in the long term. As a result, a portion of the population will mutate into fish-like creatures. Global warming and rising water levels are practical problems for the world in general and here in the Netherlands in particular".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Smithson" title="Robert Smithson">Robert Smithson</a>’s <i>Hypothetical Continent (Map of broken clear glass, Atlantis)</i> was first created as a photographical project on <a href="/wiki/Loveladies,_New_Jersey" title="Loveladies, New Jersey">Loveladies Island NJ</a> in 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> and then recreated as a gallery installation of broken glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> On this he commented that he liked “landscapes that suggest prehistory”, and this is borne out by the original conceptual drawing of the work that includes an inset map of the continent sited off the coast of Africa and at the straits into the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Plato's contemporaries pictured the world as consisting of only Europe, Northern Africa, and Western Asia (see the map of <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a>). Atlantis, according to Plato, had conquered all Western parts of the known world, making it the literary counter-image of <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persia</a>. See <cite class="citation book">Welliver, Warman (1977). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Ppg3AAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA42" rel="nofollow"><i>Character, Plot and Thought in Plato's Timaeus-Critias</i></a>. Leiden: E.J. Brill. p. 42. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-04-04870-7" title="Special:BookSources/90-04-04870-7">90-04-04870-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Character%2C+Plot+and+Thought+in+Plato%27s+Timaeus-Critias&amp;rft.place=Leiden&amp;rft.pages=42&amp;rft.pub=E.J.+Brill&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.isbn=90-04-04870-7&amp;rft.aulast=Welliver&amp;rft.aufirst=Warman&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DPpg3AAAAIAAJ%26pg%3DPA42&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtlantis"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Hackforth-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Hackforth_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hackforth, R. (1944). <li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Hartmann, Anna-Maria (2015). "The Strange Antiquity of Francis Bacon's <i>New Atlantis</i>". <i>Renaissance Studies</i>. <b>29</b> (3): 375–393. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1111%2Frest.12084" rel="nofollow">10.1111/rest.12084</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Renaissance+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=The+Strange+Antiquity+of+Francis+Bacon%27s+New+Atlantis&amp;rft.volume=29&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=375-393&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Frest.12084&amp;rft.aulast=Hartmann&amp;rft.aufirst=Anna-Maria&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtlantis"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Frame_story" title="Frame story">frame story</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Critias_(dialogue)" title="Critias (dialogue)">Critias</a></i> tells about an alleged visit of the Athenian lawmaker <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a> (c. 638 BC – 558 BC) to Egypt, where he was told the Atlantis story that supposedly occurred 9,000 years before his time.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Feder, Kenneth (2011). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=8yw5QwAACAAJ&amp;pg=PA141" rel="nofollow">"Lost: One Continent - Reward"</a>. <i>Frauds, Myths, and Mysteries: Science and Pseudoscience in Archaeology</i> (Seventh ed.). In Cleary, John J.; Gurtler, Gary M. <i>Proceedings of the Boston Area Colloquium in Ancient Philosophy</i>. <b>15</b>. Leiden: E. J. Brill. pp. 1–21. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-04-11704-0" title="Special:BookSources/90-04-11704-0">90-04-11704-0</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+Invention+of+Atlantis%3A+The+Anatomy+of+a+Fiction&amp;rft.btitle=Proceedings+of+the+Boston+Area+Colloquium+in+Ancient+Philosophy&amp;rft.place=Leiden&amp;rft.pages=1-21&amp;rft.pub=E.+J.+Brill&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.isbn=90-04-11704-0&amp;rft.aulast=Clay&amp;rft.aufirst=Diskin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DAMRl67uqD9wC%26pg%3DPA1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAtlantis"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"As Smith discusses in the opening article in this theme issue, the lost island-continent was – in all likelihood – entirely Plato’s invention for the purposes of illustrating arguments around Grecian polity. Archaeologists broadly agree with the view that Atlantis is quite simply 'utopia' (Doumas, 2007), a stance also taken by classical philologists, who interpret Atlantis as a metaphorical rather than an actual place (Broadie, 2013; Gill, 1979; Nesselrath, 2002). One might consider the question as being already reasonably solved but despite the general expert consensus on the matter, countless attempts have been made at finding Atlantis." (<a class="external text" href="http://shimajournal.org/issues/v10n2/c.-Dawson-Hayward-Introduction-Shima-v10n2.pdf" rel="nofollow">Dawson &amp; Hayward, 2016</a>)</span></li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%">Life</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
